<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="406" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg/220px-Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg/330px-Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pedestrian_signal_Switzerland_Lugano_traditional_green_20101231a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A traffic light for <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian" title="Pedestrian">pedestrians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Traffic lights</b>, also known as <b>traffic signals</b>, <b>traffic lamps</b>, <b>traffic semaphore</b>, <b>signal lights</b>, <b>stop lights</b>, <b>robots</b> (in <a href="/wiki/List_of_South_African_slang_words" title="List of South African slang words">South Africa</a>), and <b>traffic control signals</b> (in technical parlance),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> are signalling devices positioned at <a href="/wiki/Intersection_(road)" title="Intersection (road)">road intersections</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian_crossing" title="Pedestrian crossing">pedestrian crossings</a>, and other locations to control flows of traffic.</p>
<p>The world's first, manually operated gas-lit traffic signal was short lived. Installed in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> in December 1868, it exploded less than a month later, injuring<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-2">[2]</a></sup> or killing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-3">[3]</a></sup> its policeman operator. Traffic control started to seem necessary in the late 1890s and Earnest Sirrine from Chicago patented the first automated traffic control system in 1910. It used the words "STOP" and "PROCEED", although neither word lit up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traffic lights alternate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(traffic)" title="Right-of-way (traffic)">right of way</a> accorded to users by displaying lights of a standard colour (red, amber (yellow), and green) following a universal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_code" title="Colour code">colour code</a>. In the typical sequence of colour phases:</p>
<ul>
<li>The green light allows traffic to proceed in the direction denoted, if it is safe to do so and there is room on the other side of the intersection.</li>
<li>The amber (yellow) light warns that the signal is about to change to red. In a number of countries – among them the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> – a phase during which red and yellow are displayed together indicates that the signal is about to change to green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Actions required by drivers on a yellow light vary, with some jurisdictions requiring drivers to stop if it is safe to do so, and others allowing drivers to go through the intersection if safe to do so.</li>
<li>A flashing amber indication is a warning signal. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, a flashing amber light is used only at <a href="/wiki/Pelican_crossing" title="Pelican crossing">pelican crossings</a>, in place of the combined red–amber signal, and indicates that drivers may pass if no pedestrians are on the crossing.</li>
<li>The red signal prohibits any traffic from proceeding.</li>
<li>A flashing red indication is treated as a <a href="/wiki/Stop_sign" title="Stop sign">stop sign</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>In some countries traffic signals will go into a flashing mode if the Conflict Monitor detects a problem, such as a fault that tries to display green lights to conflicting traffic. The signal may display flashing yellow to the main road and flashing red to the side road, or flashing red in all directions. Flashing operation can also be used during times of day when traffic is light, such as late at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="563" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg/220px-Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg/330px-Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg/440px-Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trafiksignal_Tegelbacken_1953.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A traffic light in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> in 1953.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TrafficSignalInstallationUS1940.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="307" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/TrafficSignalInstallationUS1940.jpg/220px-TrafficSignalInstallationUS1940.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/TrafficSignalInstallationUS1940.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TrafficSignalInstallationUS1940.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The installation of a traffic signal in <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a> in December 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before traffic lights <a href="/wiki/Traffic_police" title="Traffic police">traffic police</a> controlled the flow of traffic, a well-documented example being that on <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a> in 1722.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup> Three men were given the task of directing traffic coming in and out of either <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> or <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>. Each officer would help direct traffic coming out of Southwark into London and he made sure all traffic stayed on the west end of the bridge. A second officer would direct traffic on the east end of the bridge to control the flow of people leaving London and going into Southwark.</p>
<p>On 9 December 1868,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> the first, non-electric, gas-lit traffic lights were installed outside the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Houses of Parliament</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> to control the traffic in Bridge Street, Great George Street and Parliament Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> They were proposed by the railway engineer <a href="/wiki/J._P._Knight" title="J. P. Knight">J. P. Knight</a> of Nottingham, who had adapted this idea from his design of railway signalling systems,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and constructed by the railway signal engineers of <a href="/wiki/John_Saxby" title="John Saxby">Saxby</a> &amp; Farmer. The main reason for the traffic light was that there was an overflow of horse-drawn traffic over <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Bridge" title="Westminster Bridge">Westminster Bridge</a> which forced thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedestrians" title="Pedestrians">pedestrians</a> to walk next to the Houses of Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollard08_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pollard08-11">[11]</a></sup> The design combined three <a href="/wiki/Railway_semaphore_signal" title="Railway semaphore signal">semaphore</a> arms with red and green gas lamps for night-time use, on a pillar, operated by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_constable" title="Police constable">police constable</a>. The gas lantern was manually turned by a traffic police officer, with a lever at its base so that the appropriate light faced traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The signal was 22 feet high. The light was called the semaphore and had arms that would extend horizontally that commanded drivers to "Stop" and then the arms would lower to a 45 degrees angle to tell drivers to proceed with "Caution". At night a red light would command "Stop" and a green light would mean use "Caution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although it was said to be successful at controlling traffic, its operational life was brief. It exploded on 2 January 1869, as a result of a leak in one of the gas lines underneath the pavement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> injuring<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-2">[2]</a></sup> or killing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-3">[3]</a></sup> the policeman who was operating it.</p>
<p>In the first two decades of the 20th century semaphore traffic signals, like the one in London, were in use all over the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> with each state having its own design of the device. One good example was from <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">Toledo, Ohio</a> in 1908. The words “Stop” and “Go” were in white on a green background and the lights had red and green <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a> illuminated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerosene_lamps" title="Kerosene lamps">kerosene lamps</a> for night travelers and the arms were eight feet above ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:22</sup> Controlled by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traffic_officer" title="Traffic officer">traffic officer</a> who would blow a whistle before changing the commands on this signal to help alert travelers of the change, the design was also used in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and Detroit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:23</sup> The example in Ohio was the first time America tried to use a more visible form of traffic control that evolved the use of semaphore. The device that was used in Ohio was designed based on the use of railroad signals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McShane99_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-McShane99-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1912, a traffic control device was placed on top a tower in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> at the Rue Montmartre and Grande Boulevard. This tower signal was manned by a police woman and she operated a revolving four-sided metal box on top of a glass showcase where the word “Stop” was painted in red and the word “Go” painted in white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:33</sup></p>
<p>An electric traffic light was developed in 1912 by <a href="/wiki/Lester_Wire" title="Lester Wire">Lester Wire</a>, a policeman in <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, who also used red-green lights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> On 5 August 1914, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=American_Traffic_Signal_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="American Traffic Signal Company (page does not exist)">American Traffic Signal Company</a> installed a traffic signal system on the corner of East 105th Street and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclid_Avenue_(Cleveland,_Ohio)" title="Euclid Avenue (Cleveland, Ohio)">Euclid Avenue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27–28</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It had two colors, red and green, and a <a href="/wiki/Buzzer" title="Buzzer">buzzer</a>, based on the design of James Hoge, to provide a warning for color changes. The design by James Hoge<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> allowed police and fire stations to <a href="/wiki/Traffic_signal_preemption" title="Traffic signal preemption">control the signals in case of emergency</a>. The first four-way, three-colour or traffic light was created by police officer <a href="/wiki/William_Potts" title="William Potts">William Potts</a> in <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> in 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ashville,_Ohio" title="Ashville, Ohio">Ashville</a>, Ohio claims to be the home of the oldest working traffic light in the United States, used at an intersection of public roads from 1932 to 1982 when it was moved to a local museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Many pictures of historical traffic lights appear at a Traffic Signal Trivia page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tower was the first innovation that used the three-coloured traffic signal and appeared first in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_Detroit" title="City of Detroit">City of Detroit</a>, where the first three-colored traffic light was built at the intersection of Michigan and Woodward Avenues in 1920. The man behind this three-color traffic light was police officer <a href="/wiki/William_Potts" title="William Potts">William Potts</a> of Detroit. He was concerned about how police officers at four different lights signals could not change their lights all at the same time. The answer was a third light that was coloured amber, which was the same color used on the railroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollard08_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pollard08-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:93</sup> Potts also placed a timer with the light to help coordinate a four-way set of lights in the city. The traffic tower soon used twelve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floodlights" title="Floodlights">floodlights</a> to control traffic and the reason for a tower in the first place was that at the time the intersection was one of the busiest in world, with over 20,000 vehicles daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:35</sup></p>
<p>Los Angeles installed its first automated traffic signals in October 1920 at five locations on Broadway. These early signals, manufactured by the Acme Traffic Signal Co., paired “Stop” and “Go” semaphore arms with small red and green lights. Bells played the role of today's amber or yellow lights, ringing when the flags changed—a process that took five seconds. By 1923 the city had installed 31 Acme traffic control devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The Acme semaphore traffic lights were often used in <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> <a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">Looney Tunes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merrie_Melodies" title="Merrie Melodies">Merrie Melodies</a> cartoons for comedic effect due to their loud bell.</p>
<p>The first interconnected traffic signal system was installed in <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a> in 1917, with six connected intersections controlled simultaneously from a manual switch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:32</sup> Automatic control of interconnected traffic lights was introduced March 1922 in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1922 traffic towers were beginning to be controlled by automatic timers. The first company to add timers in traffic lights was Crouse Hinds. They built railroad signals and were the first company to place timers in traffic lights in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, which was their home city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McShane99_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-McShane99-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:385</sup> The main advantage for the use of the timer was that it saved cities money by replacing traffic officers. The city of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> was able to reassign all but 500 of its 6,000 officers working on the traffic squad; this saved the city $12,500,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McShane99_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-McShane99-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:385</sup></p>
<p>After witnessing an accident between an automobile and a horse-drawn carriage, African American inventor, <a href="/wiki/Garrett_Morgan" title="Garrett Morgan">Garrett Morgan</a>, filed a U.S. patent for a traffic signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Patent No. 1,475,024<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> was granted on November 20, 1923 for Morgan's three-position traffic signal. Although the site HISTORY.com credits this signal as the ancestor of today's yellow warning light,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> earlier devices including the aforementioned Potts signal in Detroit included the yellow (amber) light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first traffic lights in Britain were deployed in <a href="/wiki/Piccadilly_Circus" title="Piccadilly Circus">Piccadilly Circus</a> in 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton" title="Wolverhampton">Wolverhampton</a> was the first British town to introduce automated traffic lights in 1927 in Princes Square at the junction of Lichfield Street and Princess Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> was the first city in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> to install traffic lights in 1928 on the intersection of Collins and Swanston Street.</p>
<p>The twelve-light system did not become available until 1928 and another feature of the light system was that hoods were placed over the light and each lens was sand-blasted to increase daytime visibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McShane99_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-McShane99-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:383</sup></p>
<p>Both the tower and semaphores were phased out by 1930. Towers were too big and obstructed traffic; semaphores were too small and drivers could not see them at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McShane99_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-McShane99-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:382</sup></p>
<p>The first traffic light in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a> was installed at <a href="/wiki/Egmore" title="Egmore">Egmore</a> Junction, <a href="/wiki/Chennai" title="Chennai">Chennai</a> in 1953. The city of <a href="/wiki/Bangalore" title="Bangalore">Bangalore</a> installed its first traffic light at Corporation Circle in 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The control of traffic lights made a big turn with the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computers" title="Computers">computers</a> in America in the 1950s. Thanks to computers, the changing of lights made Crosby's flow even quicker thanks to computerized detection. A pressure plate was placed at intersections so once a car was on the plate computers would know that a car was waiting at the red light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:135</sup> Some of this detection included knowing the number of waiting cars against the red light and the length of time waited by the first vehicle at the red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:141</sup> One of the best historical examples of computerized control of lights was in <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a> in 1952. One computer took control of 120 lights with six pressure-sensitive detectors measuring inbound and outbound traffic. The system was in place at the central business district, where the most traffic was between the downtown area and the north and northeastern parts of the city. The control room that housed the computer in charge of the system was in the basement of the City and County Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:141</sup> As computers started to evolve, traffic light control also improved and became easier. In 1967, the city of <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> was the first to use more advanced computers that were better at vehicle detection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:141</sup> Thanks to the new and better computers traffic flow moved even quicker than with the use of the tower. The computers maintained control over 159 signals in the cities through telephone lines. People praised the computers for their detection abilities. Thanks to detection computers could change the length of the green light based on the volume of waiting cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sessions_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Sessions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:143</sup> The rise of computers is the model of traffic control which is now used in the 21st century.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Timer" title="Timer">Countdown timers</a> on traffic lights were introduced in the 1990s. Timers are useful for pedestrians, to plan whether there is enough time to cross the intersection before the end of the walk phase, and for drivers, to know the amount of time before the light turns green. In the United States, timers for vehicle traffic are prohibited, but pedestrian timers are now required on new or upgraded signals on wider roadways.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_and_placement_of_colours">Types and placement of colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Types and placement of colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_variations_in_traffic_light_signalling_and_operation" title="List of variations in traffic light signalling and operation">List of variations in traffic light signalling and operation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traffic_lights.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1636" data-file-width="1660" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Traffic_lights.jpg/220px-Traffic_lights.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Traffic_lights.jpg/330px-Traffic_lights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Traffic_lights.jpg/440px-Traffic_lights.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traffic_lights.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Traffic lights can have several additional lights for filter turns or bus lanes. This one in <a href="/wiki/Warrington" title="Warrington">Warrington</a>, United Kingdom, also shows the red + yellow combination seen in a number of European countries, and a backing board with white border to increase the target value of the signal head. Improved visibility of the signal head is achieved during the night by using the retro-reflective white border.</div>
</div>
</div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sumburgh_Airport_Barrier.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Road crossing of (<a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>) A970 with <a href="/wiki/Sumburgh_Airport" title="Sumburgh Airport">Sumburgh</a> airport's runway. The movable barrier closes when aircraft land or take off.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Aspect is a term referring to the light that faces the drivers. A typical vertical traffic signal has three aspects, or lights, facing the oncoming traffic, red on top, yellow below, and green below that. Generally one aspect is illuminated at a time. In some cases, a fourth aspect, for a turn arrow for example, is below the three lights or aspects in more complicated road traffic intersections.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_aspects">Single aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Single aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(March 2010)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The simplest traffic light comprises either a single or a pair of coloured aspects that warns any user of the shared right of way of a possible conflict or danger.</p>
<ul>
<li>Flashing red: treated as a <a href="/wiki/Stop_sign" title="Stop sign">stop sign</a>. This can also signal the road is closed. In France, flashing red mandates absolute stop, at the crossing of a railway line, an airport strip, or a swing bridge.</li>
<li>Flashing yellow: caution, crossing or road hazard ahead.</li>
<li>Flashing green: varies among jurisdiction. Flashing green can give permission to go straight as well as make a left turn in front of opposing traffic (which is stopped by a steady red light), can indicate the end of a green cycle before the light changes to a solid yellow, or (as in British Columbia, Canada) indicates the intersection is a pedestrian crosswalk. In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, flashing green lights cannot be found. Instead, dual flashing orange lights are used.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dual_aspects">Dual aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dual aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(March 2010)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>These have two lights, usually mounted vertically. They are often seen at railway crossings, fire stations, and intersections of streets. They flash yellow when cross traffic is not expected, and turn red to stop traffic when cross traffic occurs (e.g., the fire engines are about to exit the station). They are also sometimes used for <a href="/wiki/Ramp_meter" title="Ramp meter">ramp metering</a>, where motorists enter a controlled-access highway during heavy traffic. Only one vehicle on the ramp proceeds when the signal shows green.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_or_more_aspects_.26_positioning_of_aspects">Three or more aspects &amp; positioning of aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Three or more aspects &amp; positioning of aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The standard traffic signal is the red light above the green, with yellow between.</p>
<p>When the traffic signal with three aspects is arranged horizontally or sideways, the arrangement depends on the <a href="/wiki/Right-_and_left-hand_traffic" title="Right- and left-hand traffic">rule of the road</a>. In right-lane countries, the sequence (from left to right) is red–yellow–green. In left-lane countries, the sequence is green–yellow–red.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2360" data-file-width="3540" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg/220px-Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg/330px-Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg/440px-Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_signal_aokibashi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Horizontally mounted signals in Japan</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG/220px-Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG/330px-Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG/440px-Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colourblind_traffic_signal.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A traffic signal in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halifax_Regional_Municipality" title="Halifax Regional Municipality">Halifax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> with specially-shaped lights to assist people with color blindness</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other signals are sometimes added for more control, such as for public transportation and right or left turns allowed only when the green arrow is illuminated or specifically prohibited if the red arrow is illuminated.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traffic_signal_cycles">Traffic signal cycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Traffic signal cycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Generally, at least one direction of traffic at an intersection has the green lights (green aspect) at any moment in the cycle. In some jurisdictions, for a brief time, all signals at an intersection show red at the same time, to clear any traffic in the intersection. The delay can depend on traffic, road conditions, the physical layout of the intersection, and legal requirements. Thus modern signals are built to allow the "all red" in an intersection, even if the feature is not used.</p>
<p>Some signals have no "all red" phase: the light turns green for cross traffic the instant the other light turns red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[note 1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations">Variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> and the Maritime provinces, lights are often arranged horizontally or sideways, but each aspect is a different shape: red is a square (larger than the normal circle) and usually in pairs at either end of the fixture, yellow is a diamond, and green is a circle. In many southern and southwestern U.S. states, most traffic signals are similarly horizontal in order to ease wind resistance during storms and hurricanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> The Canadian province of Alberta also uses this horizontal arrangement.</p>
<p>In Britain, normal traffic lights follow this sequence:</p>
<ul>
<li>Red – stop</li>
<li>Red and Amber – stop, indicating it will turn green</li>
<li>Green – start with caution, but only if the way is clear</li>
<li>Amber – stop if possible to do so without braking so sharply such as may cause an accident</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_traffic_signals_in_waterways.2C_on_railroads_for_rail_traffic">Use of traffic signals in waterways, on railroads for rail traffic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Use of traffic signals in waterways, on railroads for rail traffic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The three-aspect standard is also used at <a href="/wiki/List_of_locks_and_dams_of_the_Upper_Mississippi_River" title="List of locks and dams of the Upper Mississippi River">locks on the Upper Mississippi River</a>. Red means that another vessel is passing through. Yellow means that the lock chamber is being emptied or filled to match the level of the approaching vessel. After the gate opens, green means that the vessel may enter.</p>
<p>Railroad signals, for stopping trains in their own right of way, use the opposite positioning of the colors, the two types cannot be confused.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> That is, green on top and red below is the standard placement of the signal colors on railroad tracks.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pedestrian_and_cyclist_crossing_lights">Pedestrian and cyclist crossing lights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pedestrian and cyclist crossing lights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian_crossing" title="Pedestrian crossing">Pedestrian crossing</a></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sounds">Sounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some jurisdictions such as Australia and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> pedestrian lights are associated with a sound device, for the benefit of blind and visually impaired pedestrians. These make a slow beeping sound when the pedestrian lights are red and a continuous buzzing sound when the lights are green. In the Australian States of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>, the sound is produced in the same unit as the push buttons. This system of <a href="/wiki/Assistive_technology" title="Assistive technology">assistive technology</a> is also widely used at busy intersections in Canadian cities.</p>
<p>The United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Puffin_crossing" title="Puffin crossing">Puffin crossings</a> and their predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/Pelican_crossing" title="Pelican crossing">Pelican crossing</a>, will make a fast beeping sound to indicate that it is safe to cross the road. The beeping sound is disabled during the night time so as not to disturb any nearby residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some states in the United States, at some busy intersections, buttons will make a beeping sound for blind people. When the light changes, a speaker built into the button will play a recording to notify blind people that it is safe to cross. When the signal flashes red, the recording will start to count down with the countdown timer.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia_and_New_Zealand">Australia and New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Australia and New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Australia and New Zealand, the light sequence is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Green man: safe to cross the intersection</li>
<li>Flashing red man: continue to cross if already in the intersection, but do not start to cross</li>
<li>Red man: do not cross</li>
</ul>
<p>Some traffic lights in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a> have countdown timers for pedestrian crossing lights, usually a countdown from 30 when the red flashing man appeared. Countdown timers have been installed on some pedestrian crossing lights in <a href="/wiki/Auckland" title="Auckland">Auckland</a> counting down from 15 seconds.</p>
<p>Some traffic-light controlled junctions have a light sequence that stops all vehicular traffic at the junction at the same time, and gives pedestrians exclusive access to the intersection so that they can cross in any direction (including diagonally). This is prominently featured in the Sydney CBD outside town hall. This is known as a "<a href="/wiki/Pedestrian_scramble" title="Pedestrian scramble">pedestrian scramble</a>" or Barnes Dance in some places. In New Zealand such pedestrian crossings can be found in the central business district (CBD) of Auckland, <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christchurch" title="Christchurch">Christchurch</a>.</p>
<p>The Barnes Dance is named after an American traffic engineer, Henry A. Barnes. Barnes did not claim to have invented the system but was a strong advocate of it, having observed the difficulties his daughter faced crossing the road to get to school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several intersections in Wellington, New Zealand, have alternative green man figures. Eight intersections near Parliament have shillouettes of suffragette <a href="/wiki/Kate_Sheppard" title="Kate Sheppard">Kate Sheppard</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> while four intersections along Cuba Street have shillouetes of drag performer and LGBT rights activist <a href="/wiki/Carmen_Rupe" title="Carmen Rupe">Carmen Rupe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_standard">European standard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: European standard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg/220px-Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg/330px-Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg/440px-Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traffic_Lights_in_Vienna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bicycle traffic lights in Vienna</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The European approach to a signalized crossing is to use dual or, more rarely, a triple aspect<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> with a blackened out lens of a <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictogram</a> pedestrian. For cyclists, the same approach is used, with the lens blackened out for a bicycle frame. It is not uncommon to see lenses with both symbols on them. Most European countries use orange instead of yellow for the middle light.</p>
<p>The light sequence is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Green: safe to cross.</li>
<li>Yellow or orange: continue to cross only if unable to stop safely.</li>
<li>Flashing yellow or orange: cross with caution (often used when lights are out of order or shut down).</li>
<li>Red: do not cross.</li>
</ul>
<p>In Germany, the Czech Republic and some other Central European countries, a combination of red and orange lights is illuminated just before the green phase. Drivers are thereby cued to prepare to go (e.g., by restarting engine, if necessary, and/or shifting into a drive gear). The light sequence is as follows:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Green: safe to cross.</li>
<li>Orange: continue to cross only if unable to stop safely.</li>
<li>Flashing orange: cross with caution, obey signage (used when lights are out of order or shut down).</li>
<li>Red: do not cross.</li>
<li>Red and orange: do not cross, prepare for green.</li>
</ul>
<p>The light is blackened out with a pedestrian pictogram.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ampelm%C3%A4nnchen" title="Ampelmännchen">Ampelmännchen</a> pedestrian traffic signals have come to be seen as a nostalgic sign for the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" title="German Democratic Republic">German Democratic Republic</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations_2">Variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>2007 in Prague Roman Tic (pseudonym) of the arts collective <a href="/wiki/Ztohoven" title="Ztohoven">Ztohoven</a> has changed 50 pedestrian traffic lights to different own designs of men and women, showing different actions and emotions, and subsequently has been fined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a stride for equality, some of individual men on the traffic lights have been changed to women or couples (both heterosexual and homosexual) <a href="/wiki/Ampelm%C3%A4nnchen#Variations" title="Ampelmännchen">Ampelpärchen</a> in 2015 in Vienna, Salzburg and Linz (redone in December 2015).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_standard">British standard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: British standard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United Kingdom, Ireland, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Crown_dependencies" title="British Crown dependencies">British Crown dependencies</a> and dependent territories, and former possessions like Hong Kong two or more of the following signals are displayed to pedestrians -</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>A still image of a green walking person: cross the road</li>
<li>Flashing green walking person: continue to cross if already on the crossing but do not start to cross.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Some signals use an amber numerical countdown display, instead of the flashing green man, to show how long people already crossing have remaining before the red standing man shows, and traffic is given a green signal</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Red standing man: do not cross/do not start to cross</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>In the United Kingdom there is no direct offense committed if a pedestrian fails to obey crossing signals and many lights commonly only use two still images - a green walking person and a red standing man, this being the general case where the crossing is at a road junction and the pedestrian signals are in combination with those controlling vehicular traffic. Flashing amber lights and images at pedestrian crossings are used where the vehicular traffic lights perform the sole function of stopping road traffic to allow pedestrians to cross a road.</p>
<p>The same system is used also in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='why should "British standard" have been used in a Portuguese colony? And do we want to organize/ise this article this way? Including Hong Kong, above (March 2012)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China_standard">China standard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: China standard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Green: safe to cross.</li>
<li>Red: do not cross.</li>
<li>Yellow (solid, after green, before red): continue to cross only if unable to stop safely.</li>
<li>Flashing yellow: cross with caution (often used in low-traffic crossing or after midnight).</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_American_standard">North American standard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: North American standard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Pedestrian_crossing_light_washington_dc.ogv/854px--Pedestrian_crossing_light_washington_dc.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="62.869333333333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Pedestrian_crossing_light_washington_dc.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e4/Pedestrian_crossing_light_washington_dc.ogv/Pedestrian_crossing_light_washington_dc.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pedestrian_crossing_light_washington_dc.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pedestrian sign in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the United States, the most common aspect is the written 'walk' or 'don't walk.' In Canada, the white walking person is almost always used. Increasingly for retrofits of dual aspects and newer installations, the lower aspect formerly used for the "walk" signal (a walking person) is being replaced with a timer countdown. The raised hand pictogram first appeared in the city of Westmount, Montreal, and was invented by Alexander Heron, having observed a policeman's gloved hand controlling a crossing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The light sequence is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Green, blue, or white walking person or "walk": cross with caution (pedestrians have the right of way; motorists turning left or right must yield to pedestrians).</li>
<li>Flashing red or orange stop hand or "don't walk": do not start crossing, but continue if already in the middle of the intersection.</li>
<li>Red or orange stop hand or "don't walk": do not enter the intersection</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">U.S. state of Massachusetts</a> allows an unusual indication variation for pedestrian movement. At signalized intersections without separate pedestrian signal heads, the traffic signals may be programmed to turn red in all directions, followed by a steady display of yellow lights simultaneously with the red indications. During this red-plus-yellow indication, the intersection is closed to vehicular traffic and pedestrians may cross, usually in whatever direction they choose (this is known as a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barnes_dance" title="Barnes dance">Barnes dance</a>").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lights_for_public_transport">Lights for public transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traffic_light&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Lights for public transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="560" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG/220px-%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG/330px-%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG/440px-%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2-%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%84%D0%BE%D1%80.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Four-lamp public transport traffic light in Moscow, Russia, showing "stop" signal.</div>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1658" data-file-width="2500" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG/220px-Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG/330px-Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG/440px-Chinatown_Spadina_Toronto.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="729" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg/170px-Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg/255px-Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg/340px-Gen%C3%A8va_tram_signal_00678_729x1296.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>

A tram signal in Genèva, Switzerland.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="912" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg/220px-Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg/330px-Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg/440px-Karlsruhe_Ebertstr-Karlstr_Tramampeln.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
